It is well known that monosodium glutamate waste vouchers contain malt. Salt alkali and essence are even more harmful. Fake soy sauce deceives the world and seeks profit. Although Meng Jushu has ordered that if it is not made of salt and soy sauce, it should not be mixed with the name of soy sauce, so as to prevent the sale of soy sauce and salt tax on record, until the execution, so that the seller sells himself, and the ban becomes a mere formality, and it has evolved into the soy sauce of various places of Junliang heavy tax, the business has plummeted, and the salt is difficult to determine the amount, and at this time, the people's livelihood is declining, the purchasing power is weak, and there are many simple people with knowledge, and they cannot know that this contains toxic substances, only greedy prices are cheap, and they are deceived by it. If this continues for a long time, if we do not ban it as soon as possible, not only will the soy sauce industry be eliminated, the people in this industry will lose their jobs, and the loss of national tax will be very serious, and the people's livelihood will be greatly related. The association seems unable to remain silent. It is reasonable to unite with the related injured industry associations in various provinces to continue to present Qingting, and seek approval to quickly issue prohibition rules, and order all subordinates to strictly implement them, so as to maintain national tax and national livelihood, and the urgency of waiting for orders.

Youth Falls on Hard Times, Attempts Suicide in Huangpu River, Rescued 149

A young man from Anhui, Huang Yannian, sixteen years old this year, entered the Tongchuntang Pharmacy on Jiujiang Road two years ago to learn the trade. Recently, he was ordered by the shop owner to take money to Shanghai to procure medicine and return. The shop owner discovered that two of the medicines he procured were incorrect. Angered, the shop owner immediately fired Huang. Huang then took a boat to Shanghai on the 22nd of last month to find friends to make a living. Unexpectedly, he could not find them anywhere, and the travel expenses he had brought were used up. Helpless, Huang drifted in Shanghai, reduced to begging. He was even bullied by a beggar named Lao Yang San, who prevented him from begging for food in Shanghai and even beat him. Under the humiliation and suffering from hunger, and with no hope for the future, Huang considered suicide. He jumped into the Huangpu River near the French Concession the day before yesterday. Two detectives from the French Concession police station, Bu Zhenglong and Yang Zhongfang, happened to pass by and saw him. They immediately ordered a boatman to rescue Huang and brought him to the police station. After learning about the above situation, the Chinese and foreign officers at the police station were moved with compassion. They immediately raised funds to buy him a boat ticket to return to his hometown. Huang thanked them and left.

Liu Futang, a local resident residing at No. 14 Zhangjiatang, the southern end of Caohejing Town in the west of Shanghai, is fifty-six years old and works as a farmer. However, he was too deeply poisoned to be saved and died shortly after.

Recent Achievements of Huiren Hospital: 72-Year-Old Successfully Quits Smoking, Woman in Sixth Month of Miscarriage Recovers Health 156

Huiren Hospital, located at the intersection of Baker Road and Chengdu Road in this city, was founded by famous Chinese and German doctors. It specializes in treating opium addiction, providing obstetric services, and other departments. Recently, due to the government's strict prohibition of opium, many addicts have resolved to quit smoking to avoid legal sanctions. As a result, the number of people quitting smoking in the hospital has surged, and the beds are often full. Last month, statistics showed that fifty to sixty people were discharged after successfully quitting. Among them was a seventy-two-year-old man from Dalian who had been smoking for more than twenty years. He quit smoking in ten days and said that he felt no pain during the process, and that he felt refreshed and his appetite had increased greatly. The hospital's obstetrics department originally charged three yuan for delivery plus medication and injection fees. However, some families were unable to estimate the expenses, and unscrupulous hospitals often demanded twenty or thirty yuan or more for injections and medicines, which caused much hardship to the families. In view of this, the hospital has specifically introduced a safe delivery service, which includes all delivery procedures, medication, and injections, for only ten yuan, with no extra charges. Therefore, it is very popular among families. Dr. Hans, a German expert with thirty years of experience, has performed tens of millions of surgeries and can cure miscarriages and difficult births. During a consultation the other day, a woman in her sixth month of pregnancy had a miscarriage with severe bleeding and was unconscious and in critical condition. Dr. Hans performed surgery and restored her health within twenty minutes. She was hospitalized for two days and discharged safely. It can be said to be miraculous.

In Memory of Mr. Wang Junqing 162

— Zheng Yimei

Mr. Wang Junqing of Wuxing has passed away, and another elder statesman of the literary world has gone. I was deeply saddened by the news, and I cannot help but recall the various aspects of his life.

Mr. Junqing, whose courtesy name was Wenru, also known as Xinjiuwu, was a Mingjing during the Qing Dynasty. He was in charge of compiling and editing books at the Progressive Bookstore and the Rotary Club for many years. Later, he worked at the Zhonghua Civilization Ershu Bureau, editing and publishing books such as couplets and correspondence, which scholars regarded as authoritative. His greatest contribution was the "Shuoku," "Biji Daguan," and "Xiangyan Congshu," which he collated and compiled, adding summaries and annotations. His contribution to the compilation and organization of the national legacy is still remembered today. He also co-edited Xiangyan Zazhi with Zhang Mansun, Gao Taichi, and Zou Jiugai, which ceased publication after several issues due to financial difficulties.

Mr. Wang was tall and dignified, with sharp eyes and a kind demeanor that was easy to approach. He was especially happy to encourage younger generations. I met Mr. Wang several years ago when he was residing on Hartung Road in Shanghai. Whenever I had time, I would visit him. He would be collating and compiling selections of poetry from past dynasties at the bookstore, and we would sit knee-to-knee and talk for hours. Later, Mr. Wang built a house in Wuzhong, east of the North Temple Pagoda on Shitang Bridge, calling it "Jiuxin Yi," where he lived in seclusion. We exchanged letters once or twice a month as a matter of course. In his letters, he always inquired about my livelihood and circumstances, and his sincerity was overflowing in his words. I was moved to tears when I read them, knowing that such a fortunate encounter was not easy to come by in these declining times. Last autumn, I returned to Suzhou for my wife's family affairs. Her family was close to Jiuxin Yi, and I went to visit the place. I found that the exquisite verandas and gardens, once filled with colorful and vibrant flowers, were now full of cockscomb and goosefoot flowers. Autumn colors filled the eye. Mr. Wang strolled among them, pointing and saying to me, "You often write about fruits and flowers. Why not collect and supplement the writings into a special book?" This was the motivation for me to write a book of flower and fruit sketches. After the book was completed, I sent it to Mr. Wang. Mr. Wang praised it greatly, but in reality, it was crude and messy, and not worthy of the attention of a connoisseur. Mr. Wang came to Shanghai two months ago and honored me with a visit, but I was out and missed him. It was as if fate had deliberately withheld this last meeting. Thinking of it today, I am filled with regret.

Shen Sanbai's "Six Records of a Floating Life" is a good read for lyrical and narrative prose. It was originally a manuscript that Mr. Wang first discovered and entrusted to his friend Huang Moses to arrange for printing, thus making it widely available to the public. However, two of the records were lost, which Mr. Wang still regretted. He repeatedly searched for them and finally obtained the lost manuscripts from his fellow villagers, intending to reprint them and make them available to the world. Mr. Wang has died. I wonder if anyone will be able to complete his unfinished wish.

Mr. Wang enjoyed making riddles. In the past, there was the Ping Society in Shanghai, and Mr. Wang was a prominent member. He occasionally wrote couplets, which were always popular. One day, the senior Sun Yusheng presented the couplet "Three birds harm people: crow, sparrow, bustard," and Mr. Wang responded with "A hundred insects give way: tiger, bear, grizzly bear." His colleagues praised it as ingenious. He also occasionally wrote clock poems, such as the embroidered cloud and wild goose footprint style: "Lakes and mountains embroider the land of three heroes, clouds and rivers create a heaven of five colors." It was elegant and appropriate, unparalleled. "Embroidered cloud heaven" was the former site of the Immortal World on Four Horse Road.

Throughout his life, he wrote tens of thousands of words in the form of couplets, riddles, clock poems, as well as poems, prose, notes, and miscellanies. This spring, he entrusted me with the task of publishing them, but I was unable to do so in the end. Now that he has passed away forever, I feel deeply guilty.

Stopping Boiling Water by Ladling it Out 163

— Juan Yin

Tidbits 164

— (Yan Maode)

Many people seem to think that ambition is something innate and does not require artificial improvement. But in reality, most ambitions are hidden and need to be awakened and stimulated.

Ambition is something that is extremely sensitive to external stimuli. Once awakened, it still needs constant attention and education, just like the talent for music and art needs attention and education, otherwise it will wither and die.

If we have ambition but do not want to realize it, then our ambition will not maintain a sharp and fixed dynamic, and our talents will become dull and lose their ability.

Often lift yourself out of all troubles and sufferings and immerse yourself in a harmonious, beautiful, and good environment. This ability can truly be considered priceless.

Laogen 165

— Wang Shapa

People who walk through Zhaohui Garden and Bailinan Road Bridge know that there is a group of Russian beggars there. Because they have nothing to do, they all drift there, and every day, they only rely on begging for copper coins to make a living.

Laogen is one of them. He can speak fluent Shandong dialect, and he also understands Chinese things. According to him, "He used to work in the 'gun repair shop' of a certain warlord's army, and his salary was eighty yuan a month. He could repair pots, cannons, and rifles. Now, if there is nothing to do, there is no way to do anything. "Every day, in addition to the incense and bread, all the money he begged for is used to buy alcohol. I don't know how many times a day he drinks. They can "undeservingly" say the words "addicted to alcohol."

Yesterday, Laogen bought food at a small restaurant next door. I asked him, "Why do you like alcohol so much?" He replied, "You can't beg for money without drinking alcohol. It's so embarrassing to stretch out your hand and ask Chinese girls for money. If they say no, I won't have the face to ask again. If I eat enough wine, I will have the old face and not be afraid of embarrassment. I will pull the handlebars of the rickshaw and must ask for two copper coins."

After he finished eating, I gave him a copy of Shen Bao and asked him, "Do you know Chinese characters?" He took the newspaper, pointed to the sentences, and hummed the tune of a Western song. Everyone laughed, and I said to him, "It's not like that in the newspaper." He was very alert and immediately turned his voice and sang a "Down with the Powers" "Chinese Revolutionary Song", without a single mistake, and said, "This China has." Later, I asked him to sing another one, and he stretched out his hand and asked for five copper coins.

Little Western Paradise 166

— Written by Zhang Henshui

Chapter 17 Don't Ask About a Daughter's Body; It's Hard to Speak of Hidden Pain; Everyone Looks at the Noble Face, Sharing High Virtues

Today, I came to find him, but there was no sign of the matter of writing a letter of introduction to Qian that he spoke of. Mr. Jia promised to find iron, but he said he needed to handle it first. It was hard to find him, and only then did I get the train ticket money. As for his own, he didn't mention a word. I tried to probe his tone several times, but he was always ambiguous. I can see that he intends to deceive me. As he spoke, he took out a small handkerchief from his pocket and slowly wiped his eyes. After Zhichun guessed what had happened to him, he also felt sorry for him. He sighed and said, "Miss Yang, it's not that I'm saying you now, but you also have faults. You yourself are a downcast woman, so you shouldn't have stubbornly rejected the matchmaker and forced the Zhu family girl to be sold off. If she really becomes a concubine and saves the lives of herself, her mother, and her grandmother, that would be fine. But I think that Mr. Jia is also temporarily impulsive with sexual desire, spending one or two hundred yuan to find such a girl to relieve his boredom. If he is unhappy one day, does he still want her? But then again, if he really becomes unhappy after five or seven days and abandons Miss Zhu, it doesn't matter, at most he will ruin her body, but her life is still there. I'm afraid he will take her out of Tongguan. Can he really let Miss Zhu's grandmother and mother follow him? At that time, if he abandons Miss Zhu, this girl who has never seen the world will have no way to call for help and no way to enter. Wouldn't she be walking into a dead end?" When Huanhua heard this, she still didn't say anything. After a long time, she replied, "I think Mr. Jia likes her so much now that even if he changes his mind in the future, he will always give her a way out and will never abandon her and not ask." Imperial Incense Drifting Record 167

— Originally written by Princess Deling of the Qing Dynasty, translated by Qin Shouou

Until that evening, her thoughts had not been resolved, so that her spirit was very uneasy, so that no matter how small things, she was always weighed on the ground. The common characteristics of most old people, a kind of irritable and easily angry characteristics, she at this time had fully manifested itself. But as she was about to enter dinner, her depression suddenly released itself, because she had begun to speak, and what she said was the passage she had just hidden in her stomach. The first to speak with her was me. Her first sentence was a question, but this question was very strange, I really never expected. She asked me:

"Have you ever been beaten by anyone?"

This question simply stunned me, and I couldn't answer for a while. Have I ever been beaten by anyone? This question for me, a person who is well-off and well-fed, is unnecessary to ask, and is absolutely impossible! "Beating" This kind of punishment was originally for the servants, eunuchs and slaves. I myself admit that when I was young, because of my violent nature, whenever the servants or maids at home did something wrong and angered me, I would have to beat them a few times; and after they were beaten, they would have to kneel down and kowtow to me, thanking me for beating them, and vowing never to do it again. These things, in our view, seem to be very appropriate; but I should be beaten by someone, or is it my father and mother? But my mother is the most soft and gentle, she doesn't beat the servants, how can she beat me? Although my father was a little strict, he never raised his hand to beat me, and even a loud scolding can be said to have never happened! (381)

Idle Talk from the Banana Window 168

— Drunken Fool

All kinds of insects, fish, and shrimp: often transform. Such as A, can be transformed into B. Previously this magazine published "Whispers of Willow Catkins," saying, "Sometimes falling on people's books, it will become a bookworm. If it falls into a green grass pond, it will turn into duckweed. This is of course a matter: but a certain gentleman also made a "Debate on Willow Catkins," saying, "The sons of fish and shrimp can be transformed into locust nymphs." Also "Rotting grass turns into fireflies," there is no such thing. And it also involved the Journey to the West, saying that these things, like Sun Wukong pulling out a few hairs, can sew up something, are myths. In my opinion: these words are too narrow-minded. Not to mention others, after the Beginning of Summer, water rats can turn into crucian carp. I used to live in Suzhou, and behind my residence was a river. I personally saw a crucian carp dragging a rat tail, which had not yet transformed successfully. Also, the centipede used in the drugstore is planted with chicken feathers and hair. First, dig the soil to a foot deep, put the chicken feathers and hair inside, and then gently cover the top with soil. After about a week, open it, and many centipedes will be found inside, and the chicken feathers and hair will also be transformed into birds. Furthermore, goldfish can be grown with celestial silkworms, as the celestial silkworm told me. These are all credible and evidenced; in short, although science is now developed, these things should also be studied. They should not be dismissed as superstitious and mythical things, and despised.

The other day I read "The Nutrition of Fruits" in this magazine: it said that "the juice of peaches is the same as pears. People with fever should eat more peach juice to relieve the fever." etc.: According to the nature of peaches, which is hot, everyone knows this. The Materia Medica also says "Peaches are pungent and sour, sweet and hot, and slightly toxic. Eating too much can cause heat, and cause boils." The nature of pears is cool, clearing lung heat, and the functions are completely different. If used to treat fever, wouldn't it be treating heat with heat, which is useless and harmful? Children are purely yang in nature, and should not eat too much. In ancient times, peaches were listed as the lowest of the five fruits. That's what it means!

Small Recipes

Frozen Delicacies for Summer 169

— Mustard

In the scorching summer heat, rich and heavy foods are difficult to swallow. My wife, Peiyu, is skilled at making fruit jellies and similar desserts, which can be served as snacks or as part of a larger meal. Their refreshing coolness greatly enhances the appetite. Here are several methods for making them:

Apple Jelly: Take several apples, peel them, and slice them thinly. Soak the slices in an appropriate amount of water and simmer gently. When the fruit is soft, squeeze the juice through a cloth to filter out the solids. Add sugar and bring to a boil. Pour the mixture into a bowl and place it in the refrigerator or cold water for about half an hour until it becomes a clear jelly.

Lemon Jelly: Soak seven or eight maces of agar-agar in cold water until soft. Then, take two fresh lemons, slice them thinly with the peel on, place them in a clean pot, add several large cups of water, and boil. Strain to remove the solids. Add the agar-agar and three or four taels of sugar. Wait for the agar-agar to dissolve, then pour into a mold and surround with ice. Soon it will become lemon jelly.

Chocolate Powder Jelly: Mix rice flour, milk, and white sugar with water and steam over water to form a paste. Make another paste using fine flour and rose essence or juice using the same method. Then, make a separate paste with flour and several blocks of chocolate. Take a pre-made mold, first pour in the white paste, then the chocolate paste, and finally the rose-colored paste. Steam gently over low heat, occasionally moistening with orange juice. Place in cold water to set.

Goose Breast Jelly: Take cleaned goose breasts and soak them in a mixture of red wine, soy sauce essence, and vinegar for about three hours. Place them in a pot, add water, spices, spring onions, lemon peel, bay leaves, coriander, and other ingredients. Simmer gently until cooked. Remove and cut into square pieces. Separately, cook veal, adding red wine, soy sauce, and vinegar. Strain the juice through a cloth and pour it over the goose breasts. Place in a ventilated area or cold water. It will solidify quickly, making it an excellent appetizer for hot weather.

Chicken Breast Jelly: The method is the same as for goose breast jelly.

Mullet Jelly: Take a mullet, remove the scales and internal organs, and cut it in half. Wash it clean with water. Place it in a pot with water, vinegar, red wine, soy sauce, spices, a small amount of coriander and spring onions, bay leaves, lemon, and other ingredients. Simmer gently. After removing it, pour beef broth (with the fat removed) over it. Place it in a cool place for a short time, and it will solidify into a firm mullet jelly.

Sports News

Cochet Arrives in Shanghai – Performance Facing Difficulties – Amateurs Cannot Compete Against Professionals – Possibly Enlisting Canavarro's Help 170

Former French Davis Cup representative and world professional tennis player Cochet will come to Shanghai in early autumn to perform. He has already made arrangements with Qiu Feihai of the Shanghai Lawn Tennis Club, which has attracted much attention in Shanghai's tennis circles. However, since Cochet is now a professional player, the relationship between professional and amateur players means that Cochet's performance in Shanghai, as it stands, may not have much hope.

Greatest Difficulty

The biggest difficulty is the regulation of the International Lawn Tennis Federation, which prohibits amateur players from competing against professional players in any match, otherwise they will lose their amateur status. After the Shanghai Lawn Tennis Club received news of Cochet's arrival in Shanghai, it telegraphed the British Lawn Tennis Association to inquire whether it could hold an exhibition match with Cochet, but has not yet received a reply from London, and it is feared that it may not be allowed. As for the National Sports Federation, it is also unlikely to allow Chinese amateur players to compete against him, as it is bound by the rules of the World Amateur Sports Association.

Singapore's Side

Cochet is currently touring Malaysia and India. When he passed through Singapore, the Malaysian Lawn Tennis Association had planned to compete with Cochet, but it was abandoned because it was not allowed by the International Lawn Tennis Federation. The situation in Hong Kong is likely to be the same. With Singapore and Hong Kong in this situation, Shanghai is unlikely to be an exception.

Hopes for Coming to Shanghai

The only hope is that when Cochet comes to Shanghai in the future, he can have a match with his coach, or with Canavarro, the only professional tennis player in Shanghai. If Shanghai tennis players are willing to give up their amateur status, they may also play a match with Cochet.

Japanese tennis players, at the invitation of the China Lawn Tennis Club, competed again yesterday at the club's courts on Singapore Road. The main event was the battle between the two Lins. Lin and Qiu had no surprising tactics, and were far inferior in terms of technique.

Education News

Ministry of Education Order – Provisional Regulations for Senior Midwifery Schools 172

The Ministry of Education issued an order to the education departments and bureaus of various provinces and cities on the 4th, stating: "Upon investigation, the Provisional Regulations for Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools have been formulated by the Ministry. The previously promulgated Provisional Standards for the School System and Curriculum of Midwifery Schools should be abolished immediately. We hereby issue a copy of the Provisional Regulations for your compliance and instruct your subordinates to comply accordingly."

Provisional Regulations for Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools

1. Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools aim to train midwifery and maternal and infant health personnel. 2. In addition to the provisions of Article 7 of the Revised Vocational School Regulations, the age of admission to Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools may be set at 18 to 30 years old, depending on the circumstances. 3. The length of study at Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools is set at three years. The first academic year focuses on the study of basic subjects and observation of general work within the maternity hospital. The second academic year focuses on lecturing on specialized subjects and various basic midwifery practices, such as outpatient clinics, delivery rooms, and home visits. The third academic year is devoted to various practical exercises and management practices. 4. In addition to being implemented within the school, the third academic year's internship may also be carried out in public or private maternal and infant health and medical institutions agreed upon by the school. When serving outside the school, the school should still be responsible for guidance, and the director of the service institution should be responsible for examining service performance. 5. The number of students admitted to Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools shall not exceed twice the number of beds in its affiliated maternity hospital. The venues for the third year's internship should have sufficient preparation. 6. The subjects to be taught and the number of teaching and practical hours in each academic year of Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools are prescribed in the table below (table omitted; upon inspection, each academic year is 41 weeks, each semester is 20 and a half weeks, and the three-year curriculum and practice total 5063.5 hours, with 1148 hours of lectures and 3915.5 hours of practice). 7. Each subject in Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools emphasizes both theory and practice and is taught in Mandarin Chinese. 8. Students in Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools should be divided into groups of one to three people during the midwifery practice period and be responsible for delivery and all postpartum care matters, with a minimum of 25 times within the hospital and 5 times outside the hospital. 9. After the completion of academic and practical studies, students in Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools should be required to undergo a management practice period. In addition to practicing various management tasks in the school, they should also visit other maternal and infant health and public or private medical institutions. 10. Practical maternity hospitals should determine the number of beds based on the circumstances. For every two additional students, one bed should be added. 11. Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools may charge tuition and boarding fees in the second academic year and should reduce or waive them appropriately in the third academic year. 12. Students in Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools should wear uniforms, the style of which is prescribed as follows: (1) The material should be cloth that is washable and does not change color. (2) The style should be a dark blue long dress, round collar, white apron, white hat, and white or black shoes and socks. 13. The qualifications of the principal and teachers of Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools shall be in accordance with the provisions of Articles 90 and 92 of the Revised Vocational School Regulations. The instructors of its practical maternity hospital may be graduates of Senior Midwifery Vocational Schools with three or more years of practical experience. 14. Matters not covered in these regulations shall be handled in accordance with the Revised Vocational School Regulations.

Little Men (5) 181

— Original work by American author Louisa May Alcott, translated by Zheng Xiaocang (Zonghai)

Nat went back to his beloved book, and his sister led the new guest to a room in the back, where they saw a strong man playing and having fun with two children on the sofa, and a thin woman finishing a letter and seeming to be rereading it. (Sofa is a common translation of "Sofa.")

"He's here, Auntie!" Mei shouted.

"This is my new little boy, isn't it? I'm happy to see you, my dear, and I hope you can have a good time here," the woman said, dragging him to her side and stroking his forehead with a motherly caress and expression, making Nat's lonely little heart feel an attachment to her.

Her appearance was not beautiful, but she had a cheerful face, and she always seemed to have some childlike attitude and spirit, and her voice and demeanor were the same. These things are difficult to describe, but easy to see and feel, making her a happy and amiable person, easy to get along with, and always joyful. As she straightened his hair, she saw Nat's little lips tremble slightly, and her sharp eyes showed a touch of pity and tenderness, but she pulled the poorly dressed person closer and smiled, saying:

"I am Mrs. Bei, that gentleman is Uncle Bei, and these two are Little Bei. Children, come and meet Nat."

The three people who were twisted together immediately obeyed: the strong man with a fat child on each shoulder came to welcome the new boy. Robert and Tandy only gave him a slight smile, but Mr. Bei came to shake his hand and pointed to a small stool near the fire, saying in a sincere tone:

"A seat has been prepared for you here, my son; sit down and dry your wet feet immediately."

"Wet? That's right! My dear, take off your shoes immediately, and I can prepare something dry for you," Mrs. Bei shouted, rushing back and forth with great enthusiasm. Before Nat could speak, he found himself comfortably in a comfortable chair, with dry socks and warm slippers on his feet. So he only said, "Thank you, Mrs. Teacher," saying it so sincerely that Mrs. Bei showed a kind expression again. He was an easily moved person, and at this point, he was deeply moved, so he tried to say something cheerful.

Economic Supplement

Review of When Foreign Rice First Entered China 182

— Liu Tang

Yesterday (August 3rd), I accepted an invitation from the Chinese Youth Association to lecture on the problem of Chinese people's food. Before leaving, Mr. Yao asked me when foreign rice first entered China. This question is rather complicated and cannot be fully explained in a few words. I am happy to disclose this article for reference. Regarding the history of imported grains, my humble work has already appeared in the "History of Food Policy in China Throughout the Ages" (published by the Commercial Press). Recently, there have been two more articles: (1) "The Relationship Between Foreign Rice and Chinese People's Food" (see the July issue of New China Magazine, published by the China Book Bureau), (2) "The Exemption and Taxation of Foreign Rice" (see the July issue of Shen Bao Monthly, published by Shen Bao Newspaper Office). Combining these with this article, the details of the history of foreign rice are generally complete. In addition, the organization of imported flour and wheat has also been completed, and will be published later. Morning of the 4th.

When foreign rice first entered China, there is hardly any clear record in historical books before the Qing Dynasty. However, its entry into China certainly did not begin in the Qing Dynasty, which can be inferred. The so-called foreign rice producing areas today were either once part of our territory or our vassals. The relationship between them occurred thousands of years ago, so how could the import of grain have waited until the early Qing Dynasty to be realized? Therefore, based on the conditions of various rice-producing countries at that time, as recorded in historical books, and the changes in their relationship with our country, the question of when foreign rice first entered China may not be difficult to understand from this analysis.

The foreign rice that has the closest relationship with our country is the rice from Tokyo and Saigon, which are all produced in Annam. From the Qin and Han Dynasties to the Tang Dynasty, Annam was included in our territory. Until the early Song Dynasty, after the period of separatism of the Five Dynasties, it became independent from us. However, it still did not break away from its vassal relationship. It was not until the late Qing Dynasty, when it was annexed by France, that it became a foreign country to us. Tracing back to Emperor Wu of the Han Dynasty, who sent General Fubo, Lu Bode, to take the land of Nanyue and divide it into nine prefectures, the southernmost three prefectures—Jiaozhi, Jiuzhen, and Rinan—are the areas around present-day northern Annam (around Tokyo). According to the Book of Later Han:

"The Jiaozhi Prefecture of the Han Dynasty is 11,000 li south of Luoyang. The Jiuzhen Prefecture is 11,580 li south of Luoyang. The Rinan Prefecture is 13,400 li south of Luoyang."

The name "Rinan" was given because it was south of the sun, and the houses all faced north. The southernmost county of the five counties under its jurisdiction was "Xianglin." Xianglin was named because there were many elephants in the area (it was named Xiang Prefecture during the Qin Dynasty). In the second year of Yonghe of Emperor Shun of the Han Dynasty (137 AD), it was attacked and occupied by the outer barbarian Ou Lian and others, and it was called the Lin Yi Kingdom. Later, it became the territory of the countries of "Zhanbulao," "Champa," and "Old City," which roughly correspond to the central part of present-day Annam. Agriculture in Jiaozhi was already developed during the Han Dynasty, while Jiuzhen still lived a nomadic life, as can be seen in the biography of Ren Yan in the Book of Later Han. It is said: (Note)

"The custom of Jiuzhen is to make a living by hunting, and they do not know how to plow with oxen. The people often report to Jiaozhi for rice, which often leads to national shortages. Yan then ordered the casting of field tools and taught them to cultivate fields. Every year, they expanded the fields, and the people were well-supplied."

The soil in Rinan was also fertile, as the Book of Liang says: (Note 3)

"Lin Yi had no farmland at first. They coveted the fertile land of Rinan and often wanted to seize it. In the third year of Yonghe of Emperor Mu of Jin (347 AD), Xiahou Lan was the governor, and his oppression was even more severe. King Fan Wen of Lin Yi felt the resentment of the people and attacked Rinan with troops..."

The reason why Lin Yi wanted to seize Rinan was precisely because its land was fertile and suitable for cultivation.

The New History of the Yuan Dynasty (Note 4) also describes the morning agricultural conditions in Annam, saying:

"Its products are mostly rice and no wheat. It values sericulture and has silk cloth but does not grow cotton. Bamboo and trees form forests that block out the sun... The grass and trees do not wither in all four seasons, and there is no difference between day and night. What was called Rinan in ancient times is probably not false."

The History of the Ming Dynasty (Note 5) is even clearer, saying:

"Its territory borders the sea to the east, and across the sea to the south is Champa. To the north, it is connected to Siming and Nanning in Guangxi, and Lin'an and Yuanjiang in Yunnan. The soil is fertile, the climate is hot, and the grain harvest is abundant every year."

Emperor Taizu of the Ming Dynasty once ordered King Chen Wei of Annam (Note 6) to supply military pay to Yunnan, which was to transport 5,000 shi to Lin'an. In the twenty-seventh year, when he was attacking Longzhou, he ordered Annam to transport 80,000 shi of rice to supply the Longzhou army. In the fifth year of Yongle, the army attacked and defeated Annam. The spoils of victory were abundant, including 13.6 million shi of rice. If Annam was not abundant in products, how could it have repeatedly supplied the army, and accumulated such a large amount? As for its transportation into China, according to the History of the Song Dynasty:

"In the third year of Xiangfu, King Li Longting of Jiaozhi requested mutual trade in Yongzhou. The emperor said, 'The people on the coast often suffer from the invasion of Jiaozhou. We still only allow Gengzhou and Ru Hongzhai to help each other, as a place to control the border. Now, if they go directly to the interior, it is quite inconvenient.'"

When Emperor Taizu of the Ming Dynasty ordered the supply of rice to the Longzhou army, Annam, because of the dangerous land route, requested to transport it to Kongxiang Cave, and then ordered to transport 20,000 shi to the Jiang River along the coast, which was only half a day away from Longzhou. It can be seen that at that time, sea transportation between China and Vietnam was convenient. The two provinces of Fujian and Guangdong, which lacked rice the most, were both coastal and had early trade with the South. Therefore, the import of Annam rice was possible.

It is said that the origin of indica rice is the Champa rice that was obtained from Fujian in the fourth year of Dazhong Xiangfu of Emperor Zhenzong of the Song Dynasty (1011 AD). Therefore, the rice that existed in China was called japonica, and the rice that came from Champa was called indica in the Yangtze River basin, and sticky rice by the people of Min and Yue, because it came from Champa. However, according to Yang Xiong's dialect, "people in Jiangnan call japonica 'you,'" so japonica and indica were originally the same thing. Regardless of whether this statement is reliable or not, judging from the History of the Song Dynasty, it first says:

"Speakers said: The people of Jiangbei plant various grains, while Jiangnan specializes in planting japonica rice. Although the local customs are suitable for different crops, the ancient system also includes interplanting to prevent floods and droughts. Therefore, the emperor ordered the chief officials of Jiangnan, Liangzhe, Jinghu, Lingnan, and Fujian to encourage the people to plant more grains. For those who lack seeds of millet, wheat, sorghum, and beans, they should be provided by the prefectures and counties in Huaibei. The prefectures in Jiangnan should also plant japonica rice widely near the water and exempt them from rent."

From this, it can be seen that the planting of japonica rice in Jiangnan continues to this day and has not changed slightly. However, the area where indica rice is now planted is far greater than the area where japonica rice is planted. In all the rice-producing provinces of Fujian, Guangdong, Zhejiang, and the Yangtze River, the majority of rice production is indica rice. Therefore, if it is said that the import of indica seeds began in the time of Emperor Zhenzong of the Song Dynasty, then what was planted in the thousands of years before Emperor Zhenzong was mostly japonica rice? If so, why would they abandon japonica rice and change to planting indica rice once Champa rice was imported? If the transferring power of Champa rice is so great, why does Jiangnan still retain its habit of "specializing in planting japonica rice" and not be assimilated? This cannot be understood by reasoning, and it is difficult to understand by facts. Furthermore, the Champa rice obtained by Emperor Zhenzong of the Song Dynasty from Fujian was clearly a type of early rice. The history says: "Jianghuai and Liangzhe are slightly earlier. Even if the paddy fields do not yield a good harvest, 30,000 hu of Champa rice should be obtained and distributed to the three regions for planting. Select the people's high and dry fields to plant it, because it is early rice." It also says: "It grows without selecting the land." It can be seen that it is also dry rice. The rice cultivation in Annam, from the season, includes early rice and late rice. From the nature, there are so-called paddy rice and dry rice. However, Annamese people also mostly plant late rice, and early rice is less. Paddy rice is more, and dry rice is less. Early rice is sown in November and harvested in March of the following year, which is the dry season, so the rainfall is less and the harvest is thin. Dry rice is also the same. This is why more paddy rice and late rice are planted. Therefore, after the import of Champa rice into China, who knows that it was also abandoned by the farmers of Jiangsu and Zhejiang because the harvest was thin? Or the current early rice, which is harvested twice a year, is still credible. However, the sticky rice in Lingnan may have come from Champa, but it cannot be said that it is all Champa seeds. The communication between Champa and China was in the years of Xian De in the Zhou Dynasty, which was later. Before that, the two Yue provinces already had rice, as can be inferred from Ren Yan teaching the people of Jiuzhen to plant rice. However, the two Yue provinces and Vietnam are adjacent, and the exchange of rice seeds is actually possible. If it is said that Champa rice seeds are easy to change, and that the current indica rice is a variant of Champa rice, it is far-fetched. Moreover, there is another proof that makes the debate about "indica" and "sticky" lose its value, which is the agricultural products of the Champa Kingdom recorded in the History of the Song Dynasty:

(The Champa Kingdom...has no wheat, but has japonica rice, sago rice, beans, and sesame seeds.)

The history says that the rice of Champa is not called "indica" or "sticky," but "japonica." It can be seen that "you" and "sticky" are both local dialects of Jiangnan and Minyue. From this, it can be seen that China is not without indica rice, but the books do not record it because indica rice is a type of japonica rice, so it is not necessary to specifically mention it. Therefore, it is called Champa rice, not Champa indica rice. Rice is the general term for grains.

Furthermore, Champa, according to historical books, did not produce rice at first. Judging from the current topography, it is not a major rice-producing area. After the Lin Yi Kingdom was defeated by Zhang Dan of the Tang Dynasty, it moved to Champa and was called the Champa Kingdom. It has been said before that Lin Yi had no farmland. Champa has few plains and lives in the coastal mountains, making a living by maritime affairs, often seeking what it lacks from foreign countries, plundering sea boats (such as in the third year of Qian Dao of Emperor Xiaozong of the Song Dynasty, when it seized the goods of the people of the Great Food Kingdom and paid tribute to the Song Dynasty), or invading Annam to the north and attacking Cambodia to the south. (Note 7) Therefore, the History of the Song Dynasty uniquely recorded the sea route between Champa and China in detail, which can be a clear proof of the frequent maritime exchanges between the Song Dynasty and Champa. It says:

"The Champa Kingdom is in the southwest of China, east to the sea...north to Guangzhou takes half a month by wind, northeast to Liangzhe takes a month, northwest to Jiaozhou takes two days, and half a month by land."

Jiaozhi is adjacent to Champa, but it takes half a month by land, and only two days by sea, which shows the inconvenience of land transportation between the two places. Therefore, when King Li Huan of Jiaozhi wanted to attack Champa in the sixth year of Taiping Xingguo of Emperor Taizong of the Song Dynasty, he also built a new seaport to facilitate navigation because the mountain roads were dangerous. It is clear that Champa faces the sea and has communication and exchanges that cannot follow the land but must follow the sea. However, because it makes a living by maritime affairs, it often trades with overseas countries, so heavy goods such as rice can also be transferred by sea for convenience. Moreover, the products of all mountains and coastal areas are always insufficient to supply their livelihood, so they often seek what they lack from overseas. In the past, King Si of Lin Yi coveted the fertile land of Rinan and wanted to possess it. Champa often fought with Jiaozhi and Chenla, all for the sake of survival. It is therefore impossible to say that Champa's rice production is more than enough for self-sufficiency and can also supply others. The History of the Ming Dynasty says: (Note 8)

"The country (Champa) has no frost or snow, and all four seasons are like summer. The grass and trees are always green. The people make a living by fishing. There is no wheat, and few people work in the fields, so the harvest is thin. The people of the country all eat betel nuts and never leave their mouths all day."

It also says: (Note 9)

"During the Chenghua period of the Ming Dynasty, King Li Hao of Annam was ordered to return the land of Champa. Hao said: 'Champa is not fertile, and families have little savings, and there is no mulberry or hemp in the fields.'" From this, it can be known that the life of the Champa people is navigation and fishing, and that "the land is not fertile, and families have little savings." It can be seen that they are not a people who work hard in farming and harvesting. How can they have a large amount of rice to export overseas? Therefore, the Champa rice mentioned in the History of the Song Dynasty should be produced by Champa and transported by the Champa people (or products of Chenla). It is clear that the people of the Song Dynasty did not understand and thought it was Champa rice.

Chenla is now the southern part of Annam, a rice-producing area. The Book of Sui says: (Note 10)

"The Chenla Kingdom is southwest of Zhenyi, originally a subordinate country of Funan. It is sixty days by boat from the Rinan Prefecture... The country is mostly mountainous to the north and has water to the south. The climate is particularly hot and there is no frost or snow. The soil is suitable for sorghum and rice, and there is little millet, fruit, and vegetables, similar to Rinan and Jiuzhen."

Historians do not compare Champa with it, which shows that Champa does not engage in farming. The Book of Tang also says: (Note 11)

"The Chenla Kingdom was originally a subordinate country of Funan... Southern people call the Chenla Kingdom the Khmer Kingdom. After Shenlong, Chenla was divided into two. The southern part is close to the sea and has many slopes and swamps, called Water Chenla (i.e., southern part), and the northern part is mostly mountainous, called Land Chenla (i.e., Cambodia)."

It is also called "Rich Chenla" because its sea has pearls and treasures, and its mountains have gold, jade, and fragrant wood. However, Champa and Chenla often fought with each other. In the early years of Shaoxi of Emperor Guangzong of the Song Dynasty (Note 12), Champa was once destroyed by Chenla, so it was also called Zhanla. After that, the two countries were constantly at war until the end of the Ming Dynasty. The History of the Ming Dynasty records the rice cultivation situation in Chenla (Note 13):

"Chenla is south of Champa, and it takes three days and nights by favorable wind to reach it... The weather is always hot, and there is no frost or snow. The rice is harvested several times a year."

The exchanges between Annam, Champa, Chenla, and China were prosperous in the Song Dynasty. During the Song Dynasty, the food of the people in the provinces of Fujian and Guangdong relied on Annam rice for supply, such as the 30,000 shi of Champa rice obtained from Fujian during the Xiangfu period, which is clear evidence. By the Ming Dynasty, foreign rice was imported to avoid taxes, and southern merchant ships mostly carried rice instead of goods. (Note 15) However, if we want to trace the original source of foreign rice, we should still trace back to when China first communicated with the southern countries by sea. According to the Book of Jin:

"At first, the various countries outside the border (Note 16) traded goods from overseas. The governor of Jiaozhou and the governor of Rinan coveted profits and insulted them, reducing the price by two or three tenths."

In times of inconvenient transportation, they could only choose to trade in precious and portable items. When shipping was open and ships could carry goods, both large and small could be transferred. The Book of Liang says:

"Since the revolution of the Liang Dynasty, the overseas countries have paid tribute, and their voyages to the sea have exceeded those of previous generations."

At that time, maritime transport between China and other countries was prosperous. After the development of the Tang Dynasty, its prestige reached far and wide, and southern countries all called Chinese people "Tang people." (Note 17) Moreover, the kings of various Funan countries (i.e., present-day Siam) built large ships and traveled the sea during the transition between the Wu and Jin Dynasties in China. They repeatedly paid tribute during the Jin, Song, Qi, Liang, Sui, and Tang Dynasties, which is sufficient evidence that they had trade and mutual trade with China. Although the ancient people's ban on communication with foreign countries was very strict, it was not prohibited if it was a tribute-paying country. (Note 18) Since the Song Dynasty, mutual trade has gradually become more prosperous. All the large ports along the coast of Guangdong, Fujian, Zhejiang, and Jiangnan established customs offices to levy taxes. Overseas mutual trade has become commonplace, and foreign rice, if needed, will also be imported accordingly (some say that the import of rice is not seen in history. This is precisely because it was neglected by historians at that time. Ivory, agarwood, gold, silver, and jewels were regarded as treasures, and they were all written about in great detail. However, "rice," which is related to the people's food, was regarded as a common food and was not taken seriously by the people of the time. There is no documentary evidence, which may be the reason for this. Furthermore, the rice produced in the prefectures of Jiaozhi was regarded as our country's product before the Song Dynasty, and the old history mostly lacked records of people's livelihood and economy. This is not only true for rice. If there is transfer and supply, there is originally no need to write about it in detail, so it can only be deduced from the text.

Annam rice entered China early and is still a major source of imported rice in China. In addition to Annam rice, Siam rice can be matched with it. Siam is mentioned in our country's history in the Book of Jin, which says:

"Funan is more than 3,000 li west of Lin Yi, and it is engaged in farming. It is planted once a year and harvested three times a year."

The Book of Sui says:

"The Red Earth Kingdom is a separate species of Funan... It is warm in winter and summer, with more rain and less drought. There is no time for planting, and it is especially suitable for rice, sorghum, white beans, and black sesame."

The New History of the Yuan Dynasty says:

"Siam and Luohu are the ancient Funan countries... Luohu is south of Siam, bordering the sea. The soil of Siam is barren and not suitable for farming. The land of Luohu is flat, and the planting is abundant. The Siamese rely on it for supply. There is a big river, which reaches the sea in the southeast of Luohu from Siam. Every summer, there is yellow water, which rises into the inland river from the seaport. The farmers take the opportunity to row boats and sow seeds. The seedlings gradually rise with the water, and the seedlings also rise with the water. When the water recedes, the seedlings are ripe. There is sowing and planting, but no plowing, so the grain is abundant and cheap."

This is the reason why the grain in Siam is cheap. The History of the Ming Dynasty says:

"The Kingdom of Siam is southwest of Champa, and it takes ten days and nights by favorable wind to reach it. It is the Red Earth Kingdom of the Sui and Tang Dynasties. Later, it was divided into Luohu and Siam. The soil of Siam is barren and not suitable for farming, and Luohu has abundant land and abundant harvests. Siam relies on it for supply. During the Yuan Dynasty, Luohu was strong and annexed its land, so it was called the Siam Luohu Kingdom. In the tenth year of Hongwu, the prince came to court and was given a cast seal, with the inscription 'Seal of the King of Siam.' The country's capitals and decrees began to be called Siam."

During the Kangxi period of the Qing Dynasty, Siam paid tribute, and it was heard that the price was low, so it was ordered to transport it to Guangdong and Fujian for sale, exempting it from taxes. (Note 19) Since then, the import of Siam rice has become increasingly important. It and Annam have become a powerful supply for the food of the Chinese people, and it is also China's largest loophole.

The Book of Tang also records that there are rice-producing countries in the south:

"The Dongxie Barbarians are located hundreds of miles west of Qianzhou, south of Shou Gong Liao, west of Yizi, and north of the Barbarians. The soil is suitable for five grains, and they do not use middle farming. However, before the sky is dark, they change every year."

"The Boliyi Kingdom is in the southeastern sea of Lin Yi... The climate is hot and humid, always like the height of summer in China, and the grain ripens twice a year."

In addition, paper towels are still a supply for the food of the people in Fujian Province, which is Taiwan. Before Taiwan was ceded to Japan, it often supplied rice to the mainland. Since it was ceded to Japan, it has also been imported. As for the origin of its rice cultivation, the History of the Ming Dynasty says: (Note 20)

"Mount Jilong has green grass every year. The soil is suitable for five grains, but it is not good for paddy fields. When the grain seeds fall to the ground, they will kill them. It is said that doing good deeds helps the heavens and asks for food. After the harvest, they mark bamboo poles on the road, called inserting green. At this time, they will kill foreigners... At the end of the Wanli period, Red-haired barbarians docked their boats here and engaged in cultivation and digging due to an incident. They set up a group and called it Taiwan."

After our Chinese people developed the wilderness and worked hard to manage it, rice became the largest product of Taiwan, and now it is no longer ours.

Sugar Market: Detailed Registration of Shantou Native Sugar 186

At the end of last month, all Chaoshan sugar in Shantou suddenly received an order from the Guangdong Provincial Materials Management Office to send personnel to establish a branch office in Shantou to register native sugar and stop allocation, with the intention of establishing a public monopoly. Upon hearing this news, Chaoshan sugar merchants sent representatives to petition the Native Sugar Association to request the Shantou branch office to cancel this plan. The association forwarded the request, but the Shantou branch office replied that the registration of native sugar was being carried out under provincial orders, and the request for cancellation could not be approved. Later, the branch office relayed an instruction from the provincial office stating that the registration deadline for sugar stocks in all districts would be extended to the end of August, and that personnel would be sent to purchase the sugar. Upon hearing this, sugar merchants and producers were even more alarmed. The Native Sugar Association sent a formal letter to the Shantou branch office, again requesting a swift cancellation and requesting a suspension of registration until the matter was resolved (original text omitted). The main points were to request respect for private enterprise, to refrain from implementing a blockade policy, and to maintain a lifeline for Chaoshan sugar. Furthermore, the suspension of transportation and purchase would be a fatal blow to Chaozhou and Meizhou. They earnestly requested consideration of public sentiment and a referral of the request for cancellation to alleviate the distress of the people. It is estimated that Shantou has over 10,000 bags of sugar, aside from the 10,000 plus bags that have been shipped out. It is still difficult to predict whether the remaining sugar can be transported. It is reported that the Shantou Native Sugar Association and various sugar industry groups in Chaozhou, together representing millions of people in Chaozhou and Meizhou, are actively opposing this measure with the goal of achieving its cancellation.

Free Talk

The Writer's Style and Their Personality 189

— Wang Jicong

Generally speaking, not only do the styles of writers from different eras and schools differ in essence, but even writers from the same era and school have some degree of difference in their respective styles. In fact, Corneille and Molière were playwrights of the same era of French classicism, but one cannot say that their styles are exactly the same; Byron, Shelley, and Keats were all English Romantics of the same era, but their works each had their own characteristics; Tolstoy and Dostoevsky were also world-renowned Russian literary giants of the nineteenth century, and both can be said to be realists, but when describing characters, the former "starts from the description of the development of the protagonist's character and moves to Drama," while the latter "starts from Drama and moves to the development of the character preparing for Drama," thus showing the great difference in their styles... To explain the different styles of these writers, individualists generally seek it in the unique personality of each writer. According to them, each writer has his own unique personality, and because the personalities of the writers are different, their respective styles are also different; to understand the unique style of each writer and even the entire content and form of their works, in their opinion, the most important thing is to make an effort to study the writer's private life and his unique personality; everything else is irrelevant.

It is true that every writer must have his own unique personality, and that this unique personality is closely related to his style; but we cannot therefore say that the writer's style is determined by his unique personality, and of course we should not explain his unique style by his unique personality. The fact that the styles of the aforementioned writers differ considerably may find some superficial explanations in their respective personalities, but we cannot explain their entire style from their unique personalities. Because every writer lives in the real society to which he belongs, enjoys the material of that society, sees and hears the things of that society, and thinks about the problems of that society, so his thoughts, his creative talent, and the content of his works are all determined by the society in which he lives; as for his style, it is directly determined by the content expressed in his works, and indirectly determined by his life consciousness and the spirit of the times. Take the aforementioned writers as examples. Because Corneille and Molière both lived in France during the era of absolutism, were influenced by the same spirit of the times, and were subject to almost the same environmental domination, their styles were fundamentally the same: the style of classicism; although under this fundamental similarity, their respective styles also have some slight differences, but this difference exists in the fundamentally same style, and is constituted by the slight differences in the things they encountered and the knowledge they gained in their respective lives; the so-called unique personality cannot play an independent role here, and if we were to say that personality plays a role here, it is subordinate to the role of society. The similarities and differences in the styles of Byron and others can also be explained by the same principle. As for the difference between Dostoevsky's method of describing characters and that of Tolstoy and his contemporaries, we can even say that this is combined with the ideological content of opposing aristocratic society and opposing tradition that runs through his works; he made the following confession in his "A Weak Heart": "It is necessary to explain and describe the status, occupation, and especially the character of the characters who appear, but because many writers start writing in this way, because the novelist narrated here does not want to follow their footsteps, he is determined to start writing only from a person's current behavior." "Unwilling to follow in the footsteps of others" is a manifestation of anti-traditional thought. If someone wants to use Dostoevsky's unique personality to explain his unique style, it would probably be in vain. Everyone knows that Dostoevsky's most unique personality is gambling and being jealous, but what is the relationship between this and his descriptive method of "starting from a person's current behavior"? No relationship at all!

Those individualists who explain a writer's style by their personality are most happy to talk about Balzac. Because Balzac has a peculiar appearance and personality; his body is fat, his face is ugly, his temperament is wild, rough, and fickle; at the same time, he describes things very directly, and he often uses what people consider to be "vulgar" and "spicy" writing to express reality; therefore, general individualistic literary researchers have not hesitated to draw such a conclusion: Balzac's "vulgar" and "simple" style, his "rough" and "spicy" flavor, are completely determined by his wild and rough personality. But, in my opinion, what is most unfavorable to individualistic literary researchers is probably Balzac.

Indeed, Balzac in all his works, likes to use what people consider to be "offensive," "vulgar" words and "spicy" satirical language. Henry James once said that Balzac's pen was "rough, violent, and barbaric," and that his style was "the simplest." If viewed purely from the form, these comments may not be unreasonable. However, the so-called "vulgar" words and "spicy" satirical language, the so-called "rough, violent, and barbaric" writing styles, are not empty things, but express certain thoughts and feelings; Balzac's fondness for using "vulgar" words and "spicy" satirical language, for using "rough, violent, and barbaric" writing styles, is because he is a pioneer of realism, and he has to expose the true face of reality and show the ugliness of society, and he has to use such words and writing styles. Therefore, we say that Balzac's fondness for using such words and writing styles is determined by his social thought and his realistic creative principles. His rough and wild personality does not have a unique and significant meaning here. If we do not believe this, then we must ask: Flaubert and Zola's styles can be said to be somewhat similar to Balzac's, and "Madame Bovary" (Flaubert) and "Les Rougon-Macquart" (Zola) can even be said to have been greatly influenced by Balzac's "The Human Comedy"; in describing reality, they also wrote with sincere pens like Balzac. Therefore, in the eyes of general moralists, Zola's works are not only "vulgar" but also indecent, and even believe that families with girls should not keep his works; if Balzac's style is determined by his personality, then Flaubert and Zola, whose styles are so similar to his, should have personalities similar to his, but Flaubert's personality is reclusive, and he is not wild and rough, and Zola does not have a wild and rough personality either. He is very laborious and meticulously strives for creation -- how should this contradiction be resolved? Literary researchers who use personality to explain the writer's style, if they cannot resolve this contradiction, then their theory is shown to be of little use.

In short, the writer's style is determined by the ideological content of his works, by his life experience, and by the spirit of the times that he feels. His personality plays a secondary role here, so to understand the writer's style, one can only study his works from a social point of view. His personality and private life are only supplementary reference materials in this study. "Li Bai composes a hundred poems after a jug of wine," when studying Li Bai's style, knowing that his fondness for alcohol is helpful, but that alone is not enough, because we cannot say that Li Bai's poems are made of wine, nor can we use his fondness for alcohol to explain his style; only by examining the social consciousness expressed in his poems and his entire creative method can we fundamentally understand the problem. The study of the styles of all other writers must also be done in this way.

Mosquito Net 190

— Ivan

"Have another drink! Have another drink! Old Brother, it’s both your thirtieth birthday and your son's first birthday! A double celebration! Be quick, have another drink!"

"Old Wei, how can I compete with your capacity? Let’s just leave it at that."

The guest, his face shining with grease, was holding a large glass of wine, waiting for the host to clink glasses. He had already clinked glasses twice, and now he was forcing him to clink a third time. The host was bitterly apologizing, almost begging for mercy. But that experienced guest suddenly pulled a sly face:

"Old Sun, are you really not giving me face?"

Old Sun bitterly drank that harsh wine. The guest, seeing that his glass was empty, gulped down his own wine in one go, threw his glass back, and returned to his seat, laughing heartily.

Old Sun felt dizzy and only wanted to lie down and have a long sleep, but in order to maintain the lively atmosphere, he had no choice but to force a smile, desperately pouring drinks for others, and helplessly swallowing the drinks that others poured for him.

The wine flowed like a yellow river, ceaselessly, between the wine pot and the glasses. Dishes were served one after another, only to be quickly eaten, and hot air rose like a thin layer of mist. The lights shone dazzlingly. The electric fan swept around energetically. But everyone still had to sweat. While sweating, they had to curse and quarrel, offer toasts, and remember to pick up food and put it in their mouths. Both host and guests had to muster their spirits to eat, drink, talk, and laugh along with others.

After seeing off the guests, the host, Old Sun, held his aching head while counting the money. The Shaoxing wine alone cost six yuan and fifty cents. Touching his aching head, he sighed inwardly and felt wronged.

The next day was Sunday. Old Sun woke up from his nap, yawned dully, and his wife waited for him to finish yawning before slowly asking:

"Will you come out with me for a while?"

"I don't have time."

"What is it?"

"I have engagements tonight."

"Engagements again! Baby is covered in mosquito bites. If we don't do something, he might develop an illness. I wanted to go and buy him a mosquito net. If you're busy with engagements, I'll just go by myself."

"Where will you get the extra money?"

"There’s a cheap advertisement in the newspaper for only five yuan."

Old Sun, whose headache hadn't completely gone away, was already in a bad mood when he woke up. Hearing that his wife wanted to buy this and that, he roared:

"Five yuan! There's only five yuan left in the drawer. There's still a week until the end of the month. Go buy it! Go buy it! Shut up the mouths of the whole family!"

Having vented his anger, he put on his long gown and went out to his "engagements," taking advantage of the energy brought on by his outburst of temper.

His wife wiped away her tears, resenting the man's unpredictable temper. Raising her head, she saw a pile of silver shields, flower baskets, mirror frames, and scrolls on the table, all gifts from the previous day, emanating an air of wealth and extravagance. Her eyes then swept to the baby's cradle next to it. The baby was sleeping soundly, his face and body covered in red spots. Thinking of the mosquito net, the mother felt heartbroken. Filled with resentment, she ran over and pushed all those red, green, and shiny things onto the floor with force, making a loud crash that startled the baby in the cradle, who started crying loudly.

A Corner of the Taihang Mountains (Part Two) 191

— Lu Fen

The small mountains are planted with cypress and thorny pine, damp and cold, with a faint pine fragrance in the gentle breeze. The town stretches out eastward from the foot of the mountain.

The town is home to three or five hundred households, quite prosperous. Just take a walk through the streets and alleys and you will see many tall walls and buildings. But when you climb to a high place and look around, you can't help but be taken aback.

At the end of the town, a wisp of green smoke rises, one strand soaring into the clear sky, the other turning into wisps, carried by the breeze into the wilderness, and eventually dissipating. And in another corner, the tall building reveals its original form. The roof is gone, and the charred ruins stand tall under the blue sky. The sun shines through the terribly large window openings, so empty and lifeless, like Europe after a Mongol raid.

It turns out that this place has suffered two major disasters in the past five or six years, very unlucky. Initially, there was a "feudal collective" association called the "Heavenly Gate Society," with an emperor, empress, military advisors, generals, civil and military officials, and no shortage of soldiers and land. Their methods were, of course, not very civilized. According to old Chinese tradition, when one declares himself emperor, one must kill millions. Once they ascended to the throne, those who defied "Heaven's mandate" had to be killed. This kingdom had a remarkable law: every commoner must have a gun, except for women, the old, and the weak. All able-bodied men were drafted into the imperial army. They also had a conscription system. This was a great disaster for tenant farmers and poor farmers, who could not afford guns and had to be killed. However, the most unfortunate were the vagrants who neither "defied Heaven's mandate" nor submitted "letters of recommendation" because the royal law also stipulated: those who do not engage in proper occupations shall be killed without question. This was a truly brilliant method for eliminating consumers and alleviating unemployment.

These unusually lively days lasted for about two years. There was constant mobilization of troops and dispatching of generals. Eventually, the emperor fled, and the imperial family and their retainers also slipped away. As soon as the army arrived, it recovered the lost territory, and the fire burned bright.

Not long after, the situation stabilized, and the lieutenants returned home to clean up the mess and resume their roles as landlords. The people were also preparing to live and work in peace, but unexpectedly, residents from the eastern mountains were captured, inevitably leading to more killings and another fire. However, the residents of the western mountains were not "benevolent" either, and immediately launched a large-scale counterattack, invading the eastern mountains, killing people, and setting fires again. They not only took back their own belongings, but also carried away everything else. As a result, the surrounding area of hundreds of miles was "reduced to ashes." At that time, it would have been very appropriate to use "broken bricks and tiles, barren land for thousands of miles" in telegrams.

Now it is very peaceful. Idlers have long been slaughtered clean. Those who followed were helping the "Heroin" company directly or indirectly, and the farmers were still fighting stones on the top of the mountain. Young masters teased the women of tenant farmers, smoked "Old Hai" (heroin), and played cards. As for the masters who had served as royal generals, they were said to be playing with "maids" all day long while smoking opium.

I wonder if such a world can be compared to medieval Europe... I remember seeing a painting somewhere: a kneeling woman, her hair draped over her back, her hands stretched upwards, her eyes wide open, looking up at the sky in pain...

The painting immediately merged with the scenery in front of me.

Artificial Ice on the Market 192

— Written by Chen Jingsheng

University Days (3) (7) 193

— Written by Storm, translated by Chen Yinzhi

Just as he was looking at the menu, she sat down beside him; soon the waiter brought several plates of dishes and a bottle of champagne, and placed them in front of the two. The cork was carefully removed "Count" had never allowed champagne corks to pop; the sparkling wine flowed into the glass. The other girls’ dishes and drinks were very simple; they secretly nudged their male companions with their elbows; my attention was also completely focused on this couple. Nali held her pale face in one hand, and her other hand lay forgotten beneath a full glass of wine; "Count" was contentedly busy eating his roast sparrow and silently drinking the champagne.

"Aren't you eating, Nali?" he finally asked.

She shook her head.

He stared at her for a while. "You don't eat, that's fine," he continued quietly, "that's up to you!" Then he poured wine for himself and continued to eat his supper.

The girl picked up the glass and put it to her lips, as if she was very thirsty, and drank it all in one gulp. Her head was still lazily propped on her hand, and she didn't lift it up. She picked up the wine bottle and poured it towards the empty glass in mid-air; the wine flowed out slowly, gradually creating foam at the bottom of the glass. Her eyes looked at the wine with a disappointed expression, as if watching her life flowing out of the bottle. She simply didn't care that the wine foam flowed from the mouth of the glass to the tabletop and from the tabletop to the ground, only her other hand was still tightly holding her silky black hair.

"Beauty," a young student with a nascent beard stretched his empty glass towards her in a begging manner, and said in a low voice, "Please pour some of your leftover wine for me to drink!"

Nali didn't lift her head, but I saw the corner of her mouth twitch rapidly.

"What are you doing, "junior", what do you want?" an old student asked, who had been busy drinking all along; "Oh, haha, such a waste of good things!" he suddenly shouted, and grabbed the girl's arm.

"Count" only moved to the side a little, as the champagne flowed past him to the ground. "Let her be," he said, "her nature is like this. Isn't it, Nali?" he added, smiling at her, "Both of us are wasteful!"

She put the bottle on the table and gave him a resentful look for no reason. Then she stood up and walked towards the door leading to the hall. But he also jumped up and followed her. A barely suppressed anger was expressed on his handsome and neat face. "What are you doing!" he said in a low voice, and roughly grabbed her arm. She stopped, didn't shake off his hand, only glared at him with scorn in her shining black eyes. He held her for a while, then withdrew his hand, laughed, and sat back at the table, slowly pouring the remaining wine in the bottle into the glass. ——I saw Nali disappear through the door of the hall, among the dancing people.

My blood was boiling; I had seen everything in the place where I was. After a while, I left the friends I was talking to and went to the hall to wait for her.
